style="width:22em"><caption>Boxing</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station,_Henstridge,_Somerset,_July_1945_A29806.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Boxing Tournament in Aid of King George&#39;s Fund For Sailors at the Royal Naval Air Station, Henstridge, Somerset, July 1945 A29806.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station%2C_Henstridge%2C_Somerset%2C_July_1945_A29806.jpg/220px-Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station%2C_Henstridge%2C_Somerset%2C_July_1945_A29806.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station%2C_Henstridge%2C_Somerset%2C_July_1945_A29806.jpg/330px-Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station%2C_Henstridge%2C_Somerset%2C_July_1945_A29806.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station%2C_Henstridge%2C_Somerset%2C_July_1945_A29806.jpg/440px-Boxing_Tournament_in_Aid_of_King_George%27s_Fund_For_Sailors_at_the_Royal_Naval_Air_Station%2C_Henstridge%2C_Somerset%2C_July_1945_A29806.jpg 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="594" /></a><div>Two <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> men boxing for charity. The modern sport was codified in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Also known as</th><td>\nWestern Boxing, Pugilism <small><sup>See note.</sup><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></small></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Focus</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Punch_(combat)" title="Punch (combat)">Punching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strike_(attack)" title="Strike (attack)">striking</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Country of origin</th><td>\nPrehistoric</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Parenthood</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Bare-knuckle_boxing" title="Bare-knuckle boxing">Bare-knuckle boxing</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Olympic sport</th><td>\n688 BC (Ancient Greece)<br />1904 (modern)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Boxing</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Combat_sport" title="Combat sport">combat sport</a> in which two people, usually wearing <a href="/wiki/Boxing_glove" title="Boxing glove">protective gloves</a>, throw <a href="/wiki/Punch_(combat)" title="Punch (combat)">punches</a> at each other for a predetermined amount of time in a <a href="/wiki/Boxing_ring" title="Boxing ring">boxing ring</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Amateur_boxing" title="Amateur boxing">Amateur boxing</a> is both an <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a> sport and is a common fixture in most international games\xe2\x80\x94it also has its own World Championships. Boxing is overseen by a <a href="/wiki/Referee_(boxing)" title="Referee (boxing)">referee</a> over a series of one- to three-minute intervals called rounds. \n</p><p>The result is decided when an opponent is deemed incapable to continue by a referee, is disqualified for breaking a rule, resigns by <a href="/wiki/Submission_(combat_sports)" title="Submission (combat sports)">throwing in a towel</a>. If a fight completes all of its allocated rounds, the victor is determined by <a href="/wiki/Professional_boxing#Scoring" title="Professional boxing">judges\' scorecards</a> at the end of the contest. In the event that both fighters gain equal scores from the judges, professional bouts are considered a <a href="/wiki/Tie_(draw)" title="Tie (draw)">draw</a>. In Olympic boxing, because a winner must be declared, judges award the content to one fighter on technical criteria.\n</p><p>While humans have fought in <a href="/wiki/Hand-to-hand_combat" title="Hand-to-hand combat">hand-to-hand combat</a> since the dawn of human history, the earliest evidence of fist-fighting <a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">sporting</a> contests date back to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Middle_East" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Middle East">ancient Middle East</a> in the 3rd and 2nd millennia BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The earliest evidence of boxing rules date back to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, where boxing was established as an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic game</a> in 688 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Boxing evolved from 16th- and 18th-century prizefights, largely in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, to the forerunner of modern boxing in the mid-19th century with the 1867 introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Queensberry_Rules" title="Marquess of Queensberry Rules">Marquess of Queensberry Rules</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_boxing" title="Ancient Greek boxing">Ancient Greek boxing</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_boxers_fresco,_Akrotiri,_Greece.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Young_boxers_fresco%2C_Akrotiri%2C_Greece.jpg/150px-Young_boxers_fresco%2C_Akrotiri%2C_Greece.jpg" width="150" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Young_boxers_fresco%2C_Akrotiri%2C_Greece.jpg/225px-Young_boxers_fresco%2C_Akrotiri%2C_Greece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Young_boxers_fresco%2C_Akrotiri%2C_Greece.jpg/300px-Young_boxers_fresco%2C_Akrotiri%2C_Greece.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1848" data-file-height="3148" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_boxers_fresco,_Akrotiri,_Greece.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting of <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> youths boxing, from an <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_(Santorini)" title="Akrotiri (Santorini)">Akrotiri</a> fresco circa BCE 1650. This is the earliest documented use of <a href="/wiki/Boxing_gloves" class="mw-redirect" title="Boxing gloves">boxing gloves</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greece,_Boxers_(youths),_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ancient_Greece%2C_Boxers_%28youths%29%2C_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg/220px-Ancient_Greece%2C_Boxers_%28youths%29%2C_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ancient_Greece%2C_Boxers_%28youths%29%2C_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg/330px-Ancient_Greece%2C_Boxers_%28youths%29%2C_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ancient_Greece%2C_Boxers_%28youths%29%2C_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg/440px-Ancient_Greece%2C_Boxers_%28youths%29%2C_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg 2x" data-file-width="715" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greece,_Boxers_(youths),_Panathenaic_Amphora.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A boxing scene depicted on a Panathenaic amphora from Ancient Greece, circa 336 BC, British Museum</div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest known depiction of boxing comes from a <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a> in Iraq from the 3rd millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Later depictions from the 2nd millennium BC are found in reliefs from the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesopotamian">Mesopotamian</a> nations of <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> art from <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A relief sculpture from <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Thebes" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Thebes">Egyptian Thebes</a> (c. 1350 BCE) shows both boxers and spectators.<sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The earliest evidence for fist fighting with any kind of gloves can be found on <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> (c. 1500\xe2\x80\x931400 BCE).<sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopaediaBritannicaEntry-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> boxing was a well developed sport and enjoyed consistent popularity. In Olympic terms, it was first introduced in the 23rd <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympiad</a>, 688 BC. The boxers would wind leather thongs around their hands in order to protect themselves. There were no rounds and boxers fought until one of them acknowledged defeat or could not continue. Weight categories were not used, which meant heavyweights had a tendency to dominate. The style of boxing practiced typically featured an advanced left leg stance, with the left arm semi-extended as a guard, in addition to being used for striking, and with the right arm drawn back ready to strike. It was the head of the opponent which was primarily targeted, and there is little evidence to suggest that targeting the body was common.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boxing was a popular spectator <a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">sport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In order for the fighters to protect themselves against their opponents they wrapped <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> thongs around their fists. Eventually harder leather was used and the thong soon became a <a href="/wiki/Weapon" title="Weapon">weapon</a>. The Romans even introduced metal studs to the thongs to make the <a href="/wiki/Cestus" title="Cestus">cestus</a>. Fighting events were held at Roman <a href="/wiki/Amphitheatres" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphitheatres">Amphitheatres</a>. The Roman form of boxing was often a fight until death to please the spectators who gathered at such events. However, especially in later times, purchased slaves and trained combat performers were valuable commodities, and their lives were not given up without due consideration. Often slaves were used against one another in a circle marked on the floor. This is where the term ring came from. In <a href="/wiki/AD" class="mw-redirect" title="AD">AD</a> 393, during the Roman <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiator</a> period, boxing was abolished due to excessive brutality. It was not until the late 16th century that boxing re-surfaced in London.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_London_prize_ring_rules">Early London prize ring rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early London prize ring rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:217px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blow2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/eb/Blow2.jpg" width="215" height="286" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="215" data-file-height="286" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blow2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A straight right demonstrated in Edmund Price\'s <i>The Science of Defence: A Treatise on Sparring and Wrestling</i>, 1867</div></div></div>\n<p>Records of Classical boxing activity disappeared after the fall of the Western <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> when the wearing of weapons became common once again and interest in fighting with the fists waned. However, there are detailed records of various fist-fighting <a href="/wiki/Sports" class="mw-redirect" title="Sports">sports</a> that were maintained in different cities and provinces of Italy between the 12th and 17th centuries. There was also a sport in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Rus">ancient Rus</a> called <a href="/wiki/Russian_fist_fighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian fist fighting">Kulachniy Boy</a> or "Fist Fighting".\n</p><p>As the wearing of swords became less common, there was renewed interest in fencing with the fists. The sport would later resurface in England during the early 16th century in the form of <a href="/wiki/Bare-knuckle_boxing" title="Bare-knuckle boxing">bare-knuckle boxing</a> sometimes referred to as <b>prizefighting</b>. The first documented account of a bare-knuckle fight in England appeared in 1681 in the <i>London Protestant Mercury</i>, and the first English bare-knuckle champion was <a href="/wiki/James_Figg" title="James Figg">James Figg</a> in 1719.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts-1999_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-1999-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This is also the time when the word "boxing" first came to be used. This earliest form of modern boxing was very different. Contests in Mr. Figg\'s time, in addition to fist fighting, also contained fencing and cudgeling. On 6 January 1681, the first recorded boxing match took place in Britain when <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Monck" class="mw-redirect" title="Christopher Monck">Christopher Monck</a>, 2nd <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Albemarle" title="Duke of Albemarle">Duke of Albemarle</a> (and later <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Jamaica" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant Governor of Jamaica">Lieutenant Governor of Jamaica</a>) engineered a bout between his butler and his butcher with the latter winning the prize.\n</p><p>Early fighting had no written rules. There were no weight divisions or round limits, and no referee. In general, it was extremely chaotic. An early article on boxing was published in Nottingham, 1713, by <a href="/wiki/Parkyns_baronets" title="Parkyns baronets">Sir Thomas Parkyns</a>, a successful Wrestler from <a href="/wiki/Bunny,_Nottinghamshire" title="Bunny, Nottinghamshire">Bunny, Nottinghamshire</a>, who had practised the techniques he described. The article, a single page in his manual of wrestling and fencing, <i>Progymnasmata: The inn-play, or Cornish-hugg wrestler</i>, described a system of headbutting, punching, eye-gouging, chokes, and hard throws, not recognized in boxing today.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first boxing rules, called the Broughton\'s rules, were introduced by champion <a href="/wiki/Jack_Broughton" title="Jack Broughton">Jack Broughton</a> in 1743 to protect fighters in the ring where deaths sometimes occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Under these rules, if a man went down and could not continue after a count of 30 seconds, the fight was over. Hitting a downed fighter and grasping below the waist were prohibited. Broughton encouraged the use of \'mufflers\', a form of padded bandage or mitten, to be used in \'jousting\' or sparring sessions in training, and in exhibition matches.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg/220px-Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg/330px-Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg/440px-Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="551" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cribb_vs_Molineaux_1811.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tom_Cribb" title="Tom Cribb">Tom Cribb</a> vs <a href="/wiki/Tom_Molineaux" title="Tom Molineaux">Tom Molineaux</a> in a re-match for the heavyweight championship of England, 1811</div></div></div>\n<p>These rules did allow the fighters an advantage not enjoyed by today\'s boxers; they permitted the fighter to drop to one knee to end the round and begin the 30-second count at any time. Thus a fighter realizing he was in trouble had an opportunity to recover. However, this was considered "unmanly"<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and was frequently disallowed by additional rules negotiated by the Seconds of the Boxers.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In modern boxing, there is a three-minute limit to rounds (unlike the downed fighter ends the round rule). Intentionally going down in modern boxing will cause the recovering fighter to lose points in the scoring system. Furthermore, as the contestants did not have heavy leather gloves and wristwraps to protect their hands, they used different punching technique to preserve their hands because the head was a common target to hit full out.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (August 2018)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Boxing#Dubious" title="Talk:Boxing">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Almost all period manuals have powerful straight punches with the whole body behind them to the face (including forehead) as the basic blows.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (August 2018)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/London_Prize_Ring_Rules" title="London Prize Ring Rules">London Prize Ring Rules</a> introduced measures that remain in effect for professional boxing to this day, such as outlawing butting, gouging, scratching, kicking, hitting a man while down, holding the ropes, and using resin, stones or hard objects in the hands, and biting.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Marquess_of_Queensberry_rules_.281867.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marquess_of_Queensberry_rules_(1867)">Marquess of Queensberry rules (1867)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marquess of Queensberry rules (1867)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1867, the <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Queensberry_rules" class="mw-redirect" title="Marquess of Queensberry rules">Marquess of Queensberry rules</a> were drafted by <a href="/wiki/John_Graham_Chambers" title="John Graham Chambers">John Chambers</a> for amateur championships held at <a href="/wiki/Lillie_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Lillie Bridge">Lillie Bridge</a> in London for <a href="/wiki/Lightweight" title="Lightweight">Lightweights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middleweight" title="Middleweight">Middleweights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heavyweight" title="Heavyweight">Heavyweights</a>. The rules were published under the patronage of the <a href="/wiki/John_Sholto_Douglas,_9th_Marquess_of_Queensberry" class="mw-redirect" title="John Sholto Douglas, 9th Marquess of Queensberry">Marquess of Queensberry</a>, whose name has always been associated with them.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:320px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv/320px--Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv.jpg&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 180P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;180p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;240&quot; data-height=&quot;180&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;200984&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97002997003&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Leonard Cushing Kinetograph 1894.ogv" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv/220px--Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leonard_Cushing_Kinetograph_1894.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The June 1894 Leonard\xe2\x80\x93Cushing bout. Each of the six one-minute rounds recorded by the <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">Kinetograph</a> was made available to exhibitors for $22.50.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Customers who watched the final round saw Leonard score a knockdown.</div></div></div>\n<p>There were twelve rules in all, and they specified that fights should be "a fair stand-up boxing match" in a 24-foot-square or similar ring. Rounds were three minutes with one-minute rest intervals between rounds. Each fighter was given a ten-second count if he was knocked down, and wrestling was banned.\nThe introduction of <a href="/wiki/Boxing_gloves" class="mw-redirect" title="Boxing gloves">gloves</a> of "fair-size" also changed the nature of the bouts. An average pair of boxing gloves resembles a bloated pair of mittens and are laced up around the wrists.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\nThe gloves can be used to block an opponent\'s blows. As a result of their introduction, bouts became longer and more strategic with greater importance attached to defensive maneuvers such as slipping, bobbing, countering and angling. Because less defensive emphasis was placed on the use of the forearms and more on the gloves, the classical forearms outwards, torso leaning back stance of the bare knuckle boxer was modified to a more modern stance in which the torso is tilted forward and the hands are held closer to the face.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_19th_and_early_20th_centuries">Late 19th and early 20th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Late 19th and early 20th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Through the late nineteenth century, the martial art of boxing or prizefighting was primarily a sport of dubious legitimacy. Outlawed in England and much of the United States, prizefights were often held at gambling venues and broken up by police.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  Brawling and wrestling tactics continued, and riots at prizefights were common occurrences. Still, throughout this period, there arose some notable bare knuckle champions who developed fairly sophisticated fighting tactics.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amateur_Boxing_Club,_Porthaethwy_(7005565417).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Amateur_Boxing_Club%2C_Porthaethwy_%287005565417%29.jpg/170px-Amateur_Boxing_Club%2C_Porthaethwy_%287005565417%29.jpg" width="170" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Amateur_Boxing_Club%2C_Porthaethwy_%287005565417%29.jpg/255px-Amateur_Boxing_Club%2C_Porthaethwy_%287005565417%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Amateur_Boxing_Club%2C_Porthaethwy_%287005565417%29.jpg/340px-Amateur_Boxing_Club%2C_Porthaethwy_%287005565417%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amateur_Boxing_Club,_Porthaethwy_(7005565417).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amateur Boxing Club, Wales 1963</div></div></div>\n<p>The English case of <i><a href="/wiki/R_v._Coney" class="mw-redirect" title="R v. Coney">R v. Coney</a></i> in 1882 found that a <a href="/wiki/Bare-knuckle_boxing" title="Bare-knuckle boxing">bare-knuckle</a> fight was an <a href="/wiki/Assault_occasioning_actual_bodily_harm" title="Assault occasioning actual bodily harm">assault occasioning actual bodily harm</a>, despite the <a href="/wiki/Consensual_crime" title="Consensual crime">consent</a> of the participants. This marked the end of widespread public bare-knuckle contests in England.\n</p><p>The first world heavyweight champion under the Queensberry Rules was <a href="/wiki/James_J._Corbett" title="James J. Corbett">"Gentleman Jim" Corbett</a>, who defeated <a href="/wiki/John_L._Sullivan" title="John L. Sullivan">John L. Sullivan</a> in 1892 at the Pelican Athletic Club in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" class="mw-redirect" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first instance of film censorship in the United States occurred in 1897 when several states banned the showing of prize fighting films from the state of Nevada,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> where it was legal at the time.\n</p><p>Throughout the early twentieth century, boxers struggled to achieve legitimacy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  They were aided by the influence of promoters like <a href="/wiki/Tex_Rickard" title="Tex Rickard">Tex Rickard</a> and the popularity of great champions such as John L. Sullivan.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Boxing">Modern Boxing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern Boxing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The sport rising from illegal venues and outlawed prize fighting has become one of the largest multibillion-dollar sports today. A majority of young talent still comes from poverty-stricken areas around the world. Places like Mexico, Africa, South America, and Eastern Europe prove to be filled with young aspiring athletes who wish to become the future of boxing.  Even in the U.S., places like the inner cities of New York, and Chicago have given rise to promising young talent. According to Rubin, "boxing lost its appeal with the American middle class, and most of who boxes in modern America come from the streets and are street fighters".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules">Rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Queensberry_Rules" title="Marquess of Queensberry Rules">Marquess of Queensberry Rules</a></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Queensberry_rules" class="mw-redirect" title="Marquess of Queensberry rules">Marquess of Queensberry rules</a></i> have been the general rules governing modern boxing since their publication in 1867.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A boxing match typically consists of a determined number of three-minute rounds, a total of up to 9 to 12 rounds. A minute is typically spent between each round with the fighters in their assigned corners receiving advice and attention from their coach and staff. The fight is controlled by a referee who works within the ring to judge and control the conduct of the fighters, rule on their ability to fight safely, count knocked-down fighters, and rule on fouls.\n</p><p>Up to three judges are typically present at ringside to score the bout and assign points to the boxers, based on punches and elbows that connect, defense, knockdowns, hugging  and  other, more subjective, measures. Because of the open-ended style of boxing judging, many fights have controversial results, in which one or both fighters believe they have been "robbed" or unfairly denied a victory. Each fighter has an assigned corner of the ring, where his or her coach, as well as one or more "seconds" may administer to the fighter at the beginning of the fight and between rounds. Each boxer enters into the ring from their assigned corners at the beginning of each round and must cease fighting and return to their corner at the signalled end of each round.\n</p><p>A bout in which the predetermined number of rounds passes is decided by the judges, and is said to "go the distance". The fighter with the higher score at the end of the fight is ruled the winner. With three judges, unanimous and split decisions are possible, as are draws. A boxer may win the bout before a decision is reached through a knock-out; such bouts are said to have ended "inside the distance". If a fighter is knocked down during the fight, determined by whether the boxer touches the canvas floor of the ring with any part of their body other than the feet as a result of the opponent\'s punch and not a slip, as determined by the referee, the referee begins counting until the fighter returns to his or her feet and can continue. Some jurisdictions <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_eight_count" title="Mandatory eight count">require the referee to count to eight</a> regardless of if the fighter gets up before.\n</p><p>Should the referee count to ten, then the knocked-down boxer is ruled "knocked out" (whether unconscious or not) and the other boxer is ruled the winner by <a href="/wiki/Knockout" title="Knockout">knockout</a> (KO). A "technical knock-out" (TKO) is possible as well, and is ruled by the referee, fight doctor, or a fighter\'s corner if a fighter is unable to safely continue to fight, based upon injuries or being judged unable to effectively defend themselves. Many jurisdictions and sanctioning agencies also have a "three-knockdown rule", in which three knockdowns in a given round result in a TKO. A TKO is considered a knockout in a fighter\'s record. A "standing eight" count rule may also be in effect. This gives the referee the right to step in and administer a count of eight to a fighter that he or she feels may be in danger, even if no knockdown has taken place. After counting the referee will observe the fighter, and decide if he or she is fit to continue. For scoring purposes, a standing eight count is treated as a knockdown.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG/220px-Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG/330px-Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG/440px-Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="487" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ingemar_Johansson_and_Floyd_Pattersson_1959.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ingemar_Johansson" title="Ingemar Johansson">Ingemar Johansson</a> of Sweden KO\'s heavyweight champion <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Patterson" title="Floyd Patterson">Floyd Patterson</a>, 26 June 1959.</div></div></div>\n<p>In general, boxers are prohibited from hitting below the belt, holding, tripping, pushing, biting, or spitting. The boxer\'s shorts are raised so the opponent is not allowed to hit to the groin area with intent to cause pain or injury. Failure to abide by the former may result in a foul. They also are prohibited from kicking, head-butting, or hitting with any part of the arm other than the knuckles of a closed fist (including hitting with the elbow, shoulder or forearm, as well as with open gloves, the wrist, the inside, back or side of the hand). They are prohibited as well from hitting the back, back of the head or neck (called a "rabbit-punch") or the kidneys. They are prohibited from holding the ropes for support when punching, holding an opponent while punching, or ducking below the belt of their opponent (dropping below the waist of your opponent, no matter the distance between).\n</p><p>If a "clinch" \xe2\x80\x93 a defensive move in which a boxer wraps his or her opponents arms and holds on to create a pause \xe2\x80\x93 is broken by the referee, each fighter must take a full step back before punching again (alternatively, the referee may direct the fighters to "punch out" of the clinch). When a boxer is knocked down, the other boxer must immediately cease fighting and move to the furthest neutral corner of the ring until the referee has either ruled a knockout or called for the fight to continue.\n</p><p>Violations of these rules may be ruled "fouls" by the referee, who may issue warnings, deduct points, or disqualify an offending boxer, causing an automatic loss, depending on the seriousness and intentionality of the foul. An intentional foul that causes injury that prevents a fight from continuing usually causes the boxer who committed it to be disqualified. A fighter who suffers an accidental low-blow may be given up to five minutes to recover, after which they may be ruled knocked out if they are unable to continue. Accidental fouls that cause injury ending a bout may lead to a "no contest" result, or else cause the fight to go to a decision if enough rounds (typically four or more, or at least three in a four-round fight) have passed.\n</p><p>Unheard of in the modern era, but common during the early 20th Century in North America, a "newspaper decision (NWS)" might be made after a no decision bout had ended. A "no decision" bout occurred when, by law or by pre-arrangement of the fighters, if both boxers were still standing at the fight\'s conclusion and there was no knockout, no official decision was rendered and neither boxer was declared the winner. But this did not prevent the pool of ringside newspaper reporters from declaring a consensus result among themselves and printing a newspaper decision in their publications. Officially, however, a "no decision" bout resulted in neither boxer winning or losing. Boxing historians sometimes use these unofficial newspaper decisions in compiling fight records for illustrative purposes only. Often, media outlets covering a match will personally score the match, and post their scores as an independent sentence in their report.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_vs._amateur_boxing">Professional vs. amateur boxing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Professional vs. amateur boxing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg/230px-Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg" width="230" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg/345px-Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg/460px-Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg 2x" data-file-width="495" data-file-height="425" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87_and_Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Roberto_Dur%C3%A1n" title="Roberto Dur\xc3\xa1n">Roberto Dur\xc3\xa1n</a> (right) appeared in a book by <a href="/wiki/Prvoslav_Vuj%C4%8Di%C4%87" class="mw-redirect" title="Prvoslav Vuj\xc4\x8di\xc4\x87">Prvoslav Vuj\xc4\x8di\xc4\x87</a> (left)</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout the 17th to 19th centuries, boxing bouts were motivated by <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a>, as the fighters competed for <a href="/wiki/Prize_money" title="Prize money">prize money</a>, promoters controlled the gate, and spectators bet on the result.\nThe modern Olympic movement revived interest in amateur sports, and amateur boxing became an Olympic sport in 1908. In their current form, Olympic and other amateur bouts are typically limited to three or four rounds, scoring is computed by points based on the number of clean blows landed, regardless of impact, and fighters wear protective headgear, reducing the number of injuries, knockdowns, and knockouts.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Currently scoring blows in amateur boxing are subjectively counted by ringside judges, but the Australian Institute for Sport has demonstrated a prototype of an <a href="/wiki/Automated_Boxing_Scoring_System" title="Automated Boxing Scoring System">Automated Boxing Scoring System</a>, which introduces scoring objectivity, improves safety, and arguably makes the sport more interesting to spectators. Professional boxing remains by far the most popular form of the sport globally, though amateur boxing is dominant in Cuba and some former Soviet republics. For most fighters, an amateur career, especially at the Olympics, serves to develop skills and gain experience in preparation for a professional career. Western boxers typically participate in one Olympics and then turn pro, Cubans and other socialist countries have an opportunity to collect multiple medals.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, professional boxers were admitted in the <a href="/wiki/Boxing_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Boxing at the Summer Olympics">Olympic Games</a> and other tournaments sanctioned by <a href="/wiki/International_Boxing_Association_(amateur)" title="International Boxing Association (amateur)">AIBA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This was done in part to level the playing field and give all of the athletes the same opportunities government-sponsored boxers from socialist countries and post-Soviet republics have.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/List_of_boxing_organisations#Professional_organisations" title="List of boxing organisations">professional organizations</a> strongly opposed that decision.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amateur_boxing">Amateur boxing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Amateur boxing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Amateur_boxing" title="Amateur boxing">Amateur boxing</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicola_adams_crop.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Nicola_adams_crop.PNG/320px-Nicola_adams_crop.PNG" width="320" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Nicola_adams_crop.PNG/480px-Nicola_adams_crop.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Nicola_adams_crop.PNG 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="358" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicola_adams_crop.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nicola_Adams" title="Nicola Adams">Nicola Adams</a> is the first female boxer to win an Olympic gold medal. Here with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Kom" title="Mary Kom">Mary Kom</a> of India.</div></div></div>\n<p>Amateur boxing may be found at the collegiate level, at the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>, and in many other venues sanctioned by amateur boxing associations. Amateur boxing has a point scoring system that measures the number of clean blows landed rather than physical damage. Bouts consist of three rounds of three minutes in the Olympic and Commonwealth Games, and three rounds of three minutes in a national ABA (Amateur Boxing Association) bout, each with a one-minute interval between rounds.\n</p><p>Competitors wear protective headgear and gloves with a white strip or circle across the knuckle. There are cases however, where white ended gloves are not required but any solid color may be worn. The white end just is a way to make it easier for judges to score clean hits. Each competitor must have their hands properly wrapped, pre-fight, for added protection on their hands and for added cushion under the gloves. Gloves worn by the fighters must be twelve ounces in weight unless the fighters weigh under 165 pounds (75&#160;kg), thus allowing them to wear ten ounce gloves. A punch is considered a scoring punch only when the boxers connect with the white portion of the gloves. Each punch that lands cleanly on the head or torso with sufficient force is awarded a point. A referee monitors the fight to ensure that competitors use only legal blows. A belt worn over the torso represents the lower limit of punches \xe2\x80\x93 any boxer repeatedly landing low blows <a href="/wiki/Below_the_belt" title="Below the belt">below the belt</a> is disqualified. Referees also ensure that the boxers don\'t use holding tactics to prevent the opponent from swinging. If this occurs, the referee separates the opponents and orders them to continue boxing. Repeated holding can result in a boxer being penalized or ultimately disqualified. Referees will stop the bout if a boxer is seriously injured, if one boxer is significantly dominating the other or if the score is severely imbalanced.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Amateur bouts which end this way may be noted as "RSC" (referee stopped contest) with notations for an outclassed opponent (RSCO), outscored opponent (RSCOS), injury (RSCI) or head injury (RSCH).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_boxing">Professional boxing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Professional boxing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Professional_boxing" title="Professional boxing">Professional boxing</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg/220px-Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg/330px-Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg/440px-Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1947" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bellows_George_Dempsey_and_Firpo_1924.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Luis_%C3%81ngel_Firpo" title="Luis \xc3\x81ngel Firpo">Firpo</a> sending <a href="/wiki/Jack_Dempsey" title="Jack Dempsey">Dempsey</a> outside the ring; painting by <a href="/wiki/George_Bellows" title="George Bellows">George Bellows</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Professional bouts are usually much longer than amateur bouts, typically ranging from ten to twelve rounds, though four-round fights are common for less experienced fighters or club fighters. There are also some two-<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and three-round professional bouts,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> especially in Australia. Through the early 20th century, it was common for fights to have unlimited rounds, ending only when one fighter quit, benefiting high-energy fighters like <a href="/wiki/Jack_Dempsey" title="Jack Dempsey">Jack Dempsey</a>. Fifteen rounds remained the internationally recognized limit for championship fights for most of the 20th century until the <a href="/wiki/Boxing_in_the_1980s" title="Boxing in the 1980s">early 1980s</a>, when the death of boxer <a href="/wiki/Kim_Duk-koo" title="Kim Duk-koo">Kim Duk-koo</a> eventually prompted the <a href="/wiki/World_Boxing_Council" title="World Boxing Council">World Boxing Council</a> and other organizations sanctioning professional boxing to reduce the limit to twelve rounds.\n</p><p>Headgear is not permitted in professional bouts, and boxers are generally allowed to take much more damage before a fight is halted. At any time, the referee may stop the contest if he believes that one participant cannot defend himself due to injury. In that case, the other participant is awarded a technical knockout win. A technical knockout would also be awarded if a fighter lands a punch that opens a cut on the opponent, and the opponent is later deemed not fit to continue by a doctor because of the cut. For this reason, fighters often employ <a href="/wiki/Cutman" title="Cutman">cutmen</a>, whose job is to treat cuts between rounds so that the boxer is able to continue despite the cut. If a boxer simply quits fighting, or if his corner stops the fight, then the winning boxer is also awarded a technical knockout victory. In contrast with amateur boxing, professional male boxers have to be bare-chested.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Boxing_styles">Boxing styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Boxing styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_of_style">Definition of style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Definition of style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>"Style" is often defined as the strategic approach a fighter takes during a bout. No two fighters\' styles are alike, as each is determined by that individual\'s physical and mental attributes. Three main styles exist in boxing: <b>outside fighter</b> ("boxer"), <b>brawler</b> (or "slugger"), and <b>Inside fighter</b> ("swarmer"). These styles may be divided into several special subgroups, such as counter puncher, etc. The main philosophy of the styles is, that each style has an advantage over one, but disadvantage over the other one. It follows the <a href="/wiki/Rock-paper-scissors" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock-paper-scissors">rock-paper-scissors</a> scenario - boxer beats brawler, brawler beats swarmer, and swarmer beats boxer.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Boxer.2Fout-fighter"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boxer/out-fighter">Boxer/out-fighter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Boxer/out-fighter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg/220px-Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg/330px-Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg/440px-Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2932" data-file-height="3669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ali_NYWTS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heavyweight champion <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a> was a typical example of an out-fighter.</div></div></div>\n<p>A classic "boxer" or stylist (also known as an "out-fighter") seeks to maintain distance between himself and his opponent, fighting with faster, longer range punches, most notably the jab, and gradually wearing his opponent down. Due to this reliance on weaker punches, out-fighters tend to win by point decisions rather than by knockout, though some out-fighters have notable knockout records. They are often regarded as the best boxing strategists due to their ability to control the pace of the fight and lead their opponent, methodically wearing him down and exhibiting more skill and finesse than a brawler.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Out-fighters need reach, hand speed, reflexes, and footwork.\n</p><p>Notable out-fighters include <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Holmes" title="Larry Holmes">Larry Holmes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Calzaghe" title="Joe Calzaghe">Joe Calzaghe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilfredo_G%C3%B3mez" title="Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez">Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez</a>,  \n<a href="/wiki/Salvador_Sanchez" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvador Sanchez">Salvador Sanchez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cecilia_Br%C3%A6khus" title="Cecilia Br\xc3\xa6khus">Cecilia Br\xc3\xa6khus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gene_Tunney" title="Gene Tunney">Gene Tunney</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ezzard_Charles" title="Ezzard Charles">Ezzard Charles</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Willie_Pep" title="Willie Pep">Willie Pep</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meldrick_Taylor" title="Meldrick Taylor">Meldrick Taylor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Lopez_(boxer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ricardo Lopez (boxer)">Ricardo Lopez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Mayweather_Jr." title="Floyd Mayweather Jr.">Floyd Mayweather Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jones_Jr." title="Roy Jones Jr.">Roy Jones Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Ray_Leonard" title="Sugar Ray Leonard">Sugar Ray Leonard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Vazquez" class="mw-redirect" title="Miguel Vazquez">Miguel Vazquez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Gabriel_Mart%C3%ADnez" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergio Gabriel Mart\xc3\xadnez">Sergio "Maravilla" Mart\xc3\xadnez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitali_Klitschko" title="Vitali Klitschko">Vitali Klitschko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wladimir_Klitschko" title="Wladimir Klitschko">Wladimir Klitschko</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Rigondeaux" title="Guillermo Rigondeaux">Guillermo Rigondeaux</a>. This style was also used by fictional boxer <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Creed" title="Apollo Creed">Apollo Creed</a>.\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Boxer-puncher">Boxer-puncher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Boxer-puncher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>A boxer-puncher is a well-rounded boxer who is able to fight at close range with a combination of technique and power, often with the ability to knock opponents out with a combination and in some instances a single shot. Their movement and tactics are similar to that of an out-fighter (although they are generally not as mobile as an out-fighter),<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> but instead of winning by decision, they tend to wear their opponents down using combinations and then move in to score the knockout. A boxer must be well rounded to be effective using this style.\n</p><p>Notable boxer-punchers include <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canelo_%C3%81lvarez" title="Canelo \xc3\x81lvarez">Canelo \xc3\x81lvarez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wladimir_Klitschko" title="Wladimir Klitschko">Wladimir Klitschko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vasyl_Lomachenko" title="Vasyl Lomachenko">Vasyl Lomachenko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lennox_Lewis" title="Lennox Lewis">Lennox Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Louis" title="Joe Louis">Joe Louis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wilfredo_G%C3%B3mez" title="Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez">Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_de_la_Hoya" class="mw-redirect" title="Oscar de la Hoya">Oscar de la Hoya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archie_Moore" title="Archie Moore">Archie Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Cotto" title="Miguel Cotto">Miguel Cotto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nonito_Donaire" title="Nonito Donaire">Nonito Donaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sam_Langford" title="Sam Langford">Sam Langford</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Armstrong" title="Henry Armstrong">Henry Armstrong</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Ray_Robinson" title="Sugar Ray Robinson">Sugar Ray Robinson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Zale" title="Tony Zale">Tony Zale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Monz%C3%B3n" title="Carlos Monz\xc3\xb3n">Carlos Monz\xc3\xb3n</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Arg%C3%BCello" title="Alexis Arg\xc3\xbcello">Alexis Arg\xc3\xbcello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erik_Morales" class="mw-redirect" title="Erik Morales">Erik Morales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terry_Norris" title="Terry Norris">Terry Norris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marco_Antonio_Barrera" title="Marco Antonio Barrera">Marco Antonio Barrera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naseem_Hamed" title="Naseem Hamed">Naseem Hamed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hearns" title="Thomas Hearns">Thomas Hearns</a>.\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Counter_puncher">Counter puncher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Counter puncher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Counterpunch_(boxing)" title="Counterpunch (boxing)">Counter punchers</a> are slippery, defensive style fighters who often rely on their opponent\'s mistakes in order to gain the advantage, whether it be on the score cards or more preferably a knockout. They use their well-rounded defense to avoid or block shots and then immediately catch the opponent off guard with a well placed and timed punch. A fight with a skilled counter-puncher can turn into a war of attrition, where each shot landed is a battle in itself. Thus, fighting against counter punchers requires constant feinting and the ability to avoid telegraphing one\'s attacks. To be truly successful using this style they must have good reflexes, a high level of prediction and awareness, pinpoint accuracy and speed, both in striking and in footwork.\n</p><p>Notable counter punchers include <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Calzaghe" title="Joe Calzaghe">Joe Calzaghe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitali_Klitschko" title="Vitali Klitschko">Vitali Klitschko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evander_Holyfield" title="Evander Holyfield">Evander Holyfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Schmeling" title="Max Schmeling">Max Schmeling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Byrd" title="Chris Byrd">Chris Byrd</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_J_Corbett" class="mw-redirect" title="James J Corbett">Jim Corbett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Johnson_(boxer)" title="Jack Johnson (boxer)">Jack Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Hopkins" title="Bernard Hopkins">Bernard Hopkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laszlo_Papp" class="mw-redirect" title="Laszlo Papp">Laszlo Papp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Quarry" title="Jerry Quarry">Jerry Quarry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anselmo_Moreno" title="Anselmo Moreno">Anselmo Moreno</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Toney" title="James Toney">James Toney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Hagler" class="mw-redirect" title="Marvin Hagler">Marvin Hagler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_M%C3%A1rquez" title="Juan Manuel M\xc3\xa1rquez">Juan Manuel M\xc3\xa1rquez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humberto_Soto" title="Humberto Soto">Humberto Soto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Mayweather_Jr." title="Floyd Mayweather Jr.">Floyd Mayweather Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Mayweather" title="Roger Mayweather">Roger Mayweather</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pernell_Whitaker" title="Pernell Whitaker">Pernell Whitaker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Gabriel_Martinez" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergio Gabriel Martinez">Sergio Gabriel Martinez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Rigondeaux" title="Guillermo Rigondeaux">Guillermo Rigondeaux</a>. This style of boxing is also used by fictional boxer <a href="/wiki/Little_Mac_(Punch-Out!!)" title="Little Mac (Punch-Out!!)">Little Mac</a>.\n</p><p>Counter punchers usually wear their opponents down by causing them to miss their punches. The more the opponent misses, the faster they tire, and the psychological effects of being unable to land a hit will start to sink in. The counter puncher often tries to outplay their opponent entirely, not just in a physical sense, but also in a mental and emotional sense. This style can be incredibly difficult, especially against seasoned fighters, but winning a fight without getting hit is often worth the pay-off. They usually try to stay away from the center of the ring, in order to outmaneuver and chip away at their opponents. A large advantage in counter-hitting is the forward momentum of the attacker, which drives them further into your return strike. As such, knockouts are more common than one would expect from a defensive style.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Brawler.2Fslugger"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brawler/slugger">Brawler/slugger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Brawler/slugger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Foreman_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/George_Foreman_2009.jpg/220px-George_Foreman_2009.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/George_Foreman_2009.jpg/330px-George_Foreman_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/George_Foreman_2009.jpg/440px-George_Foreman_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Foreman_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Famous brawler <a href="/wiki/George_Foreman" title="George Foreman">George Foreman</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A brawler is a fighter who generally lacks finesse and footwork in the ring, but makes up for it through sheer punching power.  Many brawlers tend to lack mobility, preferring a less mobile, more stable platform and have difficulty pursuing fighters who are fast on their feet. They may also have a tendency to ignore combination punching in favor of continuous beat-downs with one hand and by throwing slower, more powerful single punches (such as hooks and uppercuts). Their slowness and predictable punching pattern (single punches with obvious leads) often leaves them open to counter punches, so successful brawlers must be able to absorb substantial amounts of punishment. However, not all brawler/slugger fighters are not mobile; some can move around and switch styles if needed but still have the brawler/slugger style such as <a href="/wiki/Wilfredo_G%C3%B3mez" title="Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez">Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Naseem_Hamed" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Naseem Hamed">Prince Naseem Hamed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danny_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Danny Garc\xc3\xada">Danny Garc\xc3\xada</a>.\n</p><p>A brawler\'s most important assets are power and chin (the ability to absorb punishment while remaining able to continue boxing). Examples of this style include <a href="/wiki/George_Foreman" title="George Foreman">George Foreman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Marciano" title="Rocky Marciano">Rocky Marciano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julio_C%C3%A9sar_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez">Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Duran" class="mw-redirect" title="Roberto Duran">Roberto Duran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danny_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Danny Garc\xc3\xada">Danny Garc\xc3\xada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilfredo_G%C3%B3mez" title="Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez">Wilfredo G\xc3\xb3mez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonny_Liston" title="Sonny Liston">Sonny Liston</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_L_Sullivan" class="mw-redirect" title="John L Sullivan">John L. Sullivan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Baer_(boxer)" title="Max Baer (boxer)">Max Baer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Naseem_Hamed" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Naseem Hamed">Prince Naseem Hamed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Mancini" title="Ray Mancini">Ray Mancini</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Tua" title="David Tua">David Tua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arturo_Gatti" title="Arturo Gatti">Arturo Gatti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micky_Ward" title="Micky Ward">Micky Ward</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brandon_R%C3%ADos" title="Brandon R\xc3\xados">Brandon R\xc3\xados</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruslan_Provodnikov" title="Ruslan Provodnikov">Ruslan Provodnikov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Katsidis" title="Michael Katsidis">Michael Katsidis</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Kirkland_(boxer)" title="James Kirkland (boxer)">James Kirkland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcos_Maidana" title="Marcos Maidana">Marcos Maidana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jake_LaMotta" title="Jake LaMotta">Jake LaMotta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manny_Pacquiao" title="Manny Pacquiao">Manny Pacquiao</a>, and Ireland\'s <a href="/wiki/John_Duddy" title="John Duddy">John Duddy</a>. This style of boxing was also used by <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fictional</a> boxers Rocky Balboa and James "Clubber" Lang.\n</p><p>Brawlers tend to be more predictable and easy to hit but usually fare well enough against other fighting styles because they train to take punches very well. They often have a higher chance than other fighting styles to score a knockout against their opponents because they focus on landing big, powerful hits, instead of smaller, faster attacks. Oftentimes they place focus on training on their upper body instead of their entire body, to increase power and endurance. They also aim to intimidate their opponents because of their power, stature and ability to take a punch.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Swarmer.2Fin-fighter"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Swarmer/in-fighter">Swarmer/in-fighter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Swarmer/in-fighter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg/220px-Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg" width="220" height="350" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg/330px-Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg/440px-Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg 2x" data-file-width="578" data-file-height="919" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Armstrong_1937.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Armstrong" title="Henry Armstrong">Henry Armstrong</a> was known for his aggressive, non-stop assault style of fighting.</div></div></div>\n<p>In-fighters/swarmers (sometimes called "pressure fighters") attempt to stay close to an opponent, throwing intense flurries and combinations of <a href="/wiki/Hook_(boxing)" title="Hook (boxing)">hooks</a> and uppercuts. Mainly <a href="/wiki/Mexican_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican people">Mexican</a>, Irish, <a href="/wiki/Irish-American" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish-American">Irish-American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Puerto Rican people">Puerto Rican</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mexican-American" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican-American">Mexican-American</a> boxers popularized this style. A successful in-fighter often needs a good "<a href="/wiki/Chin_(boxing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chin (boxing)">chin</a>" because swarming usually involves being hit with many <a href="/wiki/Jab" title="Jab">jabs</a> before they can maneuver inside where they are more effective. In-fighters operate best at close range because they are generally shorter and have less reach than their opponents and thus are more effective at a short distance where the longer arms of their opponents make punching awkward. However, several fighters tall for their division have been relatively adept at in-fighting as well as out-fighting.\n</p><p>The essence of a swarmer is non-stop aggression. Many short in-fighters use their stature to their advantage, employing a bob-and-weave defense by bending at the waist to slip underneath or to the sides of incoming punches. Unlike blocking, causing an opponent to miss a punch disrupts his balance, this permits forward movement past the opponent\'s extended arm and keeps the hands free to counter. A distinct advantage that in-fighters have is when throwing uppercuts, they can channel their entire bodyweight behind the punch; <a href="/wiki/Mike_Tyson" title="Mike Tyson">Mike Tyson</a> was famous for throwing devastating uppercuts. <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Hagler" class="mw-redirect" title="Marvin Hagler">Marvin Hagler</a> was known for his hard "<a href="/wiki/Chin_(boxing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chin (boxing)">chin</a>", punching power, body attack and the stalking of his opponents. Some in-fighters, like Mike Tyson, have been known for being notoriously hard to hit. The key to a swarmer is aggression, endurance, chin, and bobbing-and-weaving.\n</p><p>Notable in-fighters include <a href="/wiki/Henry_Armstrong" title="Henry Armstrong">Henry Armstrong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Pryor" title="Aaron Pryor">Aaron Pryor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julio_C%C3%A9sar_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez">Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Dempsey" title="Jack Dempsey">Jack Dempsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shawn_Porter" title="Shawn Porter">Shawn Porter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Cotto" title="Miguel Cotto">Miguel Cotto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Frazier" title="Joe Frazier">Joe Frazier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danny_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Danny Garc\xc3\xada">Danny Garc\xc3\xada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Tyson" title="Mike Tyson">Mike Tyson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manny_Pacquiao" title="Manny Pacquiao">Manny Pacquiao</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Marciano" title="Rocky Marciano">Rocky Marciano</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wayne_McCullough" title="Wayne McCullough">Wayne McCullough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Penalosa" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerry Penalosa">Gerry Penalosa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Greb" title="Harry Greb">Harry Greb</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Tua" title="David Tua">David Tua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ricky_Hatton" title="Ricky Hatton">Ricky Hatton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gennady_Golovkin" title="Gennady Golovkin">Gennady Golovkin</a>. This style was also used by the Street Fighter character Balrog.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combinations_of_styles">Combinations of styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Combinations of styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>All fighters have primary skills with which they feel most comfortable, but truly elite fighters are often able to incorporate auxiliary styles when presented with a particular challenge. For example, an out-fighter will sometimes plant his feet and counter punch, or a slugger may have the stamina to pressure fight with his power punches.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_matchups">Style matchups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boxing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Style matchups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg/220px-Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg/330px-Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg/440px-Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2738" data-file-height="1840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joe_Louis_-_Max_Schmeling_-_1936.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Joe_Louis" title="Joe Louis">Louis</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Max_Schmeling" title="Max Schmeling">Schmeling</a>, 1936</div></div></div>\n<p>There is a generally accepted rule of thumb about the success each of these boxing styles has against the others. In general, an in-fighter has an advantage over an out-fighter, an out-fighter has an advantage over a brawler, and a brawler has an advantage over an in-fighter; these form a cycle with each style being stronger relative to one, and weaker relative to another, with none dominating, as in <a href="/wiki/Rock-paper-scissors" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock-paper-scissors">rock-paper-scissors</a>. Naturally, many other factors, such as the skill level and training of the combatants, determine the outcome of a fight, but the widely held belief in this relationship among the styles is embodied in the clich\xc3\xa9 amongst boxing fans and writers that "styles make fights."\n</p><p>Brawlers tend to overcome swarmers or in-fighters because, in trying to get close to the slugger, the in-fighter will invariably have to walk straight into the guns of the much harder-hitting brawler, so, unless the former has a very good chin and the latter\'s stamina is poor, the brawler\'s superior power will carry the day. A famous example of this type of match-up advantage would be <a href="/wiki/George_Foreman" title="George Foreman">George Foreman</a>\'s knockout victory over <a href="/wiki/Joe_Frazier" title="Joe Frazier">Joe Frazier</a> in their original bout "The Sunshine Showdown".\n</p><p>Although in-fighters struggle against heavy sluggers, they typically enjoy more success against out-fighters or boxers. Out-fighters prefer a slower fight, with some distance between themselves and the opponent. The in-fighter tries to close that gap and unleash furious flurries. On the inside, the out-fighter loses a lot of his combat effectiveness, because he cannot throw the hard punches. The in-fighter is generally successful in this case, due to his intensity in advancing on his opponent and his good agility, which makes him difficult to evade. For example, the swarming Joe Frazier, though easily dominated by the slugger George Foreman, was able to create many more problems for the boxer <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a> in their three fights. <a href="/wiki/Joe_Louis" title="Joe Louis">Joe Louis</a>, after retirement, admitted that he hated being crowded, and that swarmers like untied/undefeated champ <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Marciano" title="Rocky Marciano">Rocky Marciano</a> would have caused him style problems even in his prime.\n</p><p>The boxer or out-fighter tends to be most successful against a brawler, whose slow speed (both hand and foot) and poor technique makes him an easy target to hit for the faster out-fighter. The out-fighter\'s main concern is to stay alert, as the brawler only needs to land one good punch to finish the fight. If the out-fighter can avoid those power punches, he can often wear the brawler down with fast jabs, tiring him out. If he is successful enough, he may even apply extra pressure in the later rounds in an attempt to achieve a knockout. Most classic boxers, such as Muhammad Ali, enjoyed their best successes against sluggers.\n</p><p>An example of a style matchup was the historical fight of <a href="/wiki/Julio_C%C3%A9sar_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez">Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez</a>, a swarmer or in-fighter, against <a href="/wiki/Meldrick_Taylor" title="Meldrick Taylor">Meldrick Taylor</a>, the boxer or out-fighter (see <a href="/wiki/Julio_C%C3%A9sar_Ch%C3%A1vez_vs._Meldrick_Taylor" title="Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez vs. Meldrick Taylor">Julio C\xc3\xa9sar Ch\xc3\xa1vez vs. Meldrick Taylor</a>).
